```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Fri, 11 Mar 2005 19:13:21 GMT, "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
> enlightened us:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> the primaries were held here in Ohio.

That seems to me a reasoned approach. Could be wrong but isn't it "McCain" ?

We really are betwixt the Devil and the Deep Blue Sea up here. Last 
General Election was October of 2004. Chretien (Liberal) who really had 
stayed around too long, left it to Paul Martin, waiting in the wings to 
take the Liberals through the election. Can't recall the numbers, but he 
has a minority Liberal government very dependent upon the support of the 
NDP (Labour or Socialists).

Meanwhile the newly constituted Conservatives ( a minority of 'old' 
style conservatives, Alliance/Reform - definitely right of centre, plus 
sympathy from 'active' and also 'non-active' religious groups). Throw 
into that mix the federal Party Quebecois - they'll vote as suits their 
Quebec base, so no reliable way of knowing which direction they will jump.

Myself as  a liberal with a small "l", and possibly others too, we had 
hoped for a fresh start with Martin. It was the Economist that dubbed 
him "Mr. Dithers" for his prevarication. Some he is guilty of, other 
aspects he has to be a realist, if he wants to hang on in politics.

I'll say it again, like his predecessors since Trudeau, all catholics 
with the exception of Kim Campbell (female and led the Conservative 
party after Brian Mulroney quit, followed by their getting decimated at 
the following election which introduced Chretien). Just like John Kerry, 
the catholic PMs didn't come clean about social issues - Kerry got 
clobbered in the States by the 'religious' and that included a high 
proportion of US catholics. Similarly Martin is getting up the nose of 
catholic traditionalists - that's a way of saying the Catholic Church as 
designed by its centuries old set of rules. Don't like the rules - then 
leave the Club.

Both our guys and Kerry should have clearly spelled it out, particularly 
as both Chretien and Martin are referred to as 'devout catholics'. My 
personal observation they should have said something to the following 
effect :-

"My religious beliefs are that same sex marriage is not acceptable. My 
church does not condemn homosexuals, (Forgive the sinner but not the 
sin). Similarly my religious beliefs do not allow me to condemn 
homosexuals (He that casts the first stone ......). Therefore in a 
religious context I see the word 'marriage' as having both a spiritual 
and cultural meaning.

I would not endorse any legislation that attacks homosexuals or 
lesbians. They must be accorded all the rights of partnerships in 
something we can define as a 'Civil Union'. In fact, as happens in some 
European countries, it is the 'Civil Union' which is the state 
recognition of marriage, followed by those of a religious persuasion 
having an official religious ceremony afterwards.

As a politician, although obviously influenced by my religious beliefs, 
I have a duty to my personal constituency to represent their views. 
Should the majority of my voters express a definite change to the 
meaning of the word 'marriage', then although reluctantly, I must 
represent my constituents. However I would be happier if we can agree on 
the dual usage of the words 'marriage' and 'Civil Union' ".

I'm no speech writer - but hope above conveys message.

Now some evangelists, catholics, other Christian groups, Muslims, Sikhs, 
Hindus, Jews etc., might still find the above difficult to swallow. But 
just perhaps, some would see the honesty in the man's words and may well 
adapt their own attitudes.

When I was a kid, homosexuality was a criminal offence, going back 
centuries. Recall the scene from Mel Gibson's 'Bravheart' where Edward I 
(Longshanks) gives his son's boy-friend the heave-ho from the castle 
window ? So 50s/60s it was no longer a criminal offence in UK. We took a 
gulp, thought on it and subsequently saw the injustice which had been 
cured by the new legislation. However, so far as 'religionists' are 
concerned, the airy-fairy approach of politicians has got them suspect.
They see changing the word 'marriage' to mean EXACTLY the same as 'civil 
union' as being a wedge. They question, "What comes next to attack our 
concept of family values ?'

Activists would have us believe that there are a reasonably large number 
of homosexuals. A study done in Chicago contradicts that saying that in 
fact (numbers escape me), that the percentage is only 0.1 or 0.01 
percent. Similarly studies have been done over roughly the last ten 
years in Scandinavia. Even though same sex marriage is allowed in some 
of the countries, it hasn't been taken up as a popular lifestyle, nor as 
sometimes implied, is there longevity to same-sex partnerships, even 
allowing for all the counter arguments about family unit divorces.

The issue which really concerns the sociologists that did this study 
(not a bunch of religious freaks), is the social time-bomb. Not now, nor 
next ten years, but somewhere down the road Gudrun with her two Daddies 
or Sven with his two Mommies is going to get curious and ask about 
"hers" or "his" family. "Where did I come from ?"  "Out of a test tube, 
Gudrun/Sven".  Real neat sci-fi scenario.

Boy am I in a quandary. First and last Tory I voted for was Winston 
Churchill - I'm having great problems sticking with Martin, but I'm not 
a happy camper with our Canadian Tories either - but on this particular 
issue, while I don't agree with their element of 'extremists', (they 
aren't all rednecks), I'm seeing myself reluctantly veering to the Right.

There are other issues with Martin - but the one above coupled with an 
active judiciary (our Supreme Court interpreting our Bill of Rights to 
make judicial rather than parliamentary law), really sticks in my craw.

Worth noting the difference between the US system and parliamentary 
systems. Like it or lump it, George W. Bush is around until the end of 
2008 - other than death, impeachment or resignation.

Paul Martin could be gone by the end of March. If he introduced some 
Bill that the various opposition groups strongly disagreed with, and 
assuming NDP (Labour) supported him - given that enough of his own 
Liberals voted with the opposition - on a vote of non-confidence - then 
he is gonzo. That's not about to happen - but that's how easily a 
parliamentary 'king' is toppled.

PS: Skippy - different topic. I see you made reference to time overseas. 
  Was it you in Cyprus handling both Greek and Turkish - if you confirm 
'Yes' I'll raise a separate thread to quiz you.

Jimmy
```

LX-i wrote:
> James J. Gavan wrote:
> 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

A joke, but with an element of truth. Something I saw a short while 
back, but can't remember specifically. Historically, as humans we do 
things in cycles. While not blatantly apparent, there is a current 
tendency to move towards the right in political circles. The move to the 
left in Europe, say 30's - 60/70's resolved many of the social issues 
which had been around since the Victorian Industrial Revolution.

No massive inequities for blue collar workers now - then not much appeal 
for Socialist politics. In UK the TUC is no longer a major force in 
politics and in the States, although of a nuisance value, groups like 
the AFCIO (have I got the initials correct ?), don't have appeal.

But NOTE ! I said Socialist not Liberal. There's a man I meet when 
walking the dogs, not on a regular basis, because we don't both walk to 
a set time. Some five years younger than me, a Romanian who has never 
been back since he left. (In his early childhood they had a Fascist 
regime with sympathy for the Nazis as Uncle Joe was looking over there 
shoulders from the East, then WWII finishes - the commies were in. The 
Soviet Empire ceased to exist - so a new crowd takes over - he says they 
are all crooks).

A trained engineer, likes history - so that's my attraction and he can 
fill me in on some of the stuff from an Eastern European angle. He is 
also conversant with English and European history, generally. Speaks 
excellent accented English, but does have some difficulty getting his 
way around certain words because of his false teeth - I'm not joking. 
The teeth are a bit of an impediment.

Sensing one day that he was a liberal, or close, I asked him outright. 
He smiled and replied with a nod :-

"Yes, I suppose so. I oscillate between the Conservatives and Liberals, 
considering their points-of-view and take features from both".

The point is, he is a centralist, not dogmatic to one party creed - 
making his own judgments and arriving at a compromise position. Think on 
it, that's what most voters really want - a vanilla flavour which is 
netiher right nor left - but attempts to statisfy all. Well, at least 
the majority. ("Majority" does NOT mean 52%, but something like 70% plus).

You aint there yet on the last two paragraphs kiddo :-)

Jimmy

Howard Brazee wrote:
> On 14-Mar-2005, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
> 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> Mid East?

First, Israel is not the #2 bad guy - Iran is.  Israel doesn't even come 
close.  Second, you're changing the subject.  The point is that the "No 
WMD!  No WMD!" crowed may soon be proved to be wrong.

But, to answer your question, we're safer *and* less safe at the same 
time.  These parts, under coordination of Saddam Hussein, could have 
produced very harmful weapons quickly.  In pieces, smaller quantities of 
weapons can be made, and it will take longer.  The downside is that if 
the pieces are all in one place, once you find them, you've eliminated 
the threat - with them dispersed, it's more difficult to eliminate *all* 
the threats.
```

They didn't show us the fertilizer, and the regime had a penchant for 
producing chemical weapons - the only safe assumption is that they were 
*not* making fertilizer.  Remember, we didn't just spin the globe, throw 
a dart at it, and decide we're going to reshape Iraq in our image.  This 
is a proven dangerous regime who routinely raped, tortured, and murdered 
its own citizens; who trained terrorists; and who has shown that they 
have no qualms about using chemical or biological weapons.

They're not on trial for petty larceny - the stakes are much higher, and

http://www.nytimes.com/2005/03/13/international/middleeast/13loot.html

Online page 4, first paragraph...
"In its most recent report to the United Nations Security Council, in 
October, the agency said it 'continues to be concerned about the 
widespread and apparently systematic dismantlement that has taken place 
at sites previously relevant to Iraq's nuclear program.'"

Online page 4, next-to-last paragraph...
"In interviews, officials of the monitoring commission and the atomic 
energy agency said the two agencies had heard nothing from Baghdad - 
with one notable exception. On Oct. 10, the Iraqi Ministry of Science 
and Technology wrote to the atomic agency to say a stockpile of high 
explosives at Al Qaqaa had been lost because of 'theft and looting.'"

It's being called looting because that's what the oh-so-honest Iraqi 
government, who did not report to the IAEA and UNMOVIC as required, is 
calling it.  As tightly as these materials must have been guarded, to 
keep the inspectors from finding it, do you honestly believe that the 
government knew nothing of this?  Somehow, they didn't notice huge 
trucks and cranes "systematic[ally] dismantling" their chemical weapons 
plants?

citizen's fight back against invader's it is not terrorism. It is the 
same thing you are paid to do, and claim a moral obligation to do. You 
are *not* fighting terrorism, you are fighting a war that *you* declared.

The only laws the US seems to want to defend are the ones that work out 
to the US owning everything, the US dictating everyone's actions, and 
the US being answerable to no one.  The other laws are for failures(like 
#42, as you put it).

jce wrote:
> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message

>>resolution?

Don't hold your breath. The Chinese are never going to volunteer to
support anything attacking anybody showing a semblance of being a
dictatorial regime. It's just as likely ex KGB-man Putnin will never 
come on board either.

Remember this thread contains 'Foreign Policy'. Daniel, you are way off
track in your optimism. We've already been there with the Chinese, and 
at the time they were a pretty primitive nation. The only time the UN
military force had significance, while I was still in apprentice
training - the Korean War of the 'Fifties, commanded by Douglas
MacArthur. Like those graphical images we see in modern movies, the
Chinese came in droves into S. Korea. They wiped our asses, just with
unlimited manpower. It finished in an uneasy stalemate, and the Korean
situation is still the same today.

Then through their surrogate, N. Vietnam, YOU (not us), had the tragedy
of the Vietnam War, with a disillusioned America leaving with its tail
between its legs.

As regards the PRC imploding, try and get a copy of Fortune "Inside the 
New China", special issue 2004 Oct 04. With permission, I took my copy 
from the doctor's waiting room :-). They are becoming a powerhouse, and 
just as in Hong Kong, they are prepared to be not quite so communistic 
ally rigid where they can make money. A new entrepreneurial class, both 
home-grown and returnees from the States are the new middle class 
driving force.

Provided the Comintern can keep the rising middle class happy, they can 
go on for decades with their slightly revamped version of capitalism. No 
good making money if you can't spend it, so they are having to consider 
'benefits' to keep the masses happy. A projection of some 2 million 
autos in China by about 2010 (or was that 2020 ?). That's a lot of 
fill-ups at the gas pumps.

I cut out the article from the Calgary Herald - but regret can't find 
it. Written by an Assistant (Chinese) Political Scientist professor at 
the U. of Alberta in Edmonton. Not surprisingly, he speaks the lingo. 
I've referred to his article before. The Chinese military, thinking war 
games, have produced a plan for attacking the USA around 2008. The 
reasoning - while their economy is growing it is blatantly inefficient.
Not accurate - but a small example - it takes us in N. America say 1 
barrel of oil to produce 100 widgets. Currently the Chinese may be using 
5 barrels of oil to get the same 100 widgets.

They are aggressively courting oil producers, Middle East, Venezuela and 
up here in the Great White North. They are direct competitors to the USA 
for the same resource. The military visualize they will have to attack 
the USA to protect the safety of their own oil imports. Meanwhile, 
Petro-China is searching for oil deposits offshore etc.

It is just as improbable that the Chinese would go nuclear any more than 
the USA. Whoever presses the first button is signaling the end of 
civilization. That rather tends to put the boot to Star Wars 2, aka BMD 
(Ballistic Missile Defense). Down at the ranch Paul Martin along with 
GWB and Vicente Fox is, as we write, probably explaining to the Prez why 
he flip-flopped on this issue.

To me BMD is a non-starter :-

1. Nobody would dare go nuclear - except for that rogue in N.Korea
2. It wont work - try getting a 100% hit rate on all incoming missiles
3. Whatever budget figure the manufacturers or military come up with - 
multiply the $BILLIONS by a factor of 5 or 6 or....10. The cost will be 
astronomical to the US.
4. Canada was being asked to contribute - perhaps only 5-10% of the cost 
- BUT - 5-10% of $x BILLIONS.

So we are back to some sort of conventional war, if there is such an 
animal. That implies ground troops with the US having to 'kick a lot of 
butt'. I would suggest your legs are really going to ache before you 
have kicked sufficient butts. You kill a million PRC troops. "No 
problem", says the Central Committee, "We'll feed in one million more".

The Chinese grabbed Tibet, a great tragedy for Tibetans, and in the past 
had border skirmishes with the Indians. Currently they have shown no 
expansionist policies, matching the Nazi or Soviet patterns. Agreed they 
do not accept the policy of the 'Two Chinas', noisily shaking their 
sword in its scabbard over Formosa. And I think they mean business on 
that one. It's a 'homeland thing', not very different from the Japanese 
objecting to the Soviets, (now Russia) occupying those Japanese northern 
islands after WWII, or the latest, South Korea disputing Japanese claims 
to some remote uninhabitable islands situated between the two.

Forget the Afghan and Iraq sideshows - concentrate on a diplomatic 
solution between the USA and the PRC. A while back, Bill pointed out to 
three of us OO-ers that WG4 (ISO) had received requests from the Ukraine 
and China to comment on the OO Collections TR(Technical Report). For all 
I'm likely to use it, that section of a revised J4 Standard might just 
as well be published in Chinese !

I responded to Bill's message along the following lines. The 20th 
Century was America's century. I predict that the 21st century will be 
China's century.

I wont be around, and you wont be around in 2099 to prove me wrong !

Jimmy

"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:e06ee$42439131$45491f85$4979@KNOLOGY.NET...

> > The US people wouldn't recognize a good democracy if one got up and bit
it
> > on the rear end.

> Yes we would - that's why we're glad we have a constitutional republic!

I must *strenuously* agree with you here.  Despite its title, the French
Republic is much more a democracy than the US, and from my three visits to
Paris I can certainly see why Jefferson was concerned about the "tyranny of
the majority".  (I can also see, after visiting Versailles, why the French
people demanded democracy!).

Because of the rules for the Initiative and Referendum process, it is
probably easier in California for just about anybody to draft a state
constitutional amendment and get sufficient signatures on the petition to
get it on the state ballot.  Frequently there will be multiple initiatives
on the same ballot that override each other.  My personal policy is to vote
NO on all statewide ballot measures unless I'm *really certain* of their
validity because my observation is that almost all of them turn out to be
cockamamie at best.  There are a whole lot of really cockamamie laws in
California.  I think there are a whole lot *more* really cockamamie laws in
France.

    -Chuck Stevens

```

On 18-Mar-2005, "jce" <defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:

> > BTW, if it prompts you for a login, use "joebleaux" as the user id and
> > "booger" as the password.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> try.

The problem with passwords is a serious problem.   We can't use passwords that
are easy to remember, and we can't write them down and post them next to our
computer.   What happens is often people go to a site that wants a password, and
they try a dozen variations of their user-id until they get one that hasn't been
used at that site before, log on, get a password, forget it, and repeat next
time they need to go there.    Or if they can, they use the same password
everywhere.   (I wonder how many sites have been created that are designed to
harvest such passwords).

In the industry, we are being pulled in many directions.   Privacy and security
are important (although the Department of Homeland Security might have other
goals here).     But making people remember dozens of different random
mixed-case with special character passwords doesn't work.

I took a course from someone using Vax's.   The Vax checked to make sure we
didn't use English words in our passwords.   She didn't mind, she was French.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Passwords

_(reply depth: 46)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2005-03-18T20:36:15+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d1fe3v0ge0@news2.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<255j215394bc0u68ci6m9sde4vvc2g8oqh@4ax.com> <1HC_d.159830$qB6.40683@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <d1evfn$ofh$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9T7QryK9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```

In article <9T7QryK9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>, l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) writes:
> .    On  18.03.05
>   wrote  howard@brazee.net (Howard Brazee)
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> HB> post them next to our computer.

Of course there's a ton of research on passwords and other forms of
shared-secret (and secret-and-verifier) authentication in computer
security.  And the conclusion everyone comes to - unless they just
adopt it as an axiom to begin with - is that passwords, particularly
short passwords, simply do not work.  They're a terrible mechanism.

(PINs are even worse.  They're much too short, and they make other
attacks, like account scanning, possible.  (In account scanning you
pick a PIN and try it across the whole range of account numbers.
Since there's only one login failure per account, the bank doesn't
lock access to any of the accounts.  With a small PIN number space
and a lot of accounts, chances of finding a match are very good.)
And ATM cards contain the PIN in the clear anyway, so if you have
a card all you need is a mag-stripe reader.  Pathetic.)

Pass *phrases* are a small improvement.  A passphrase that's not too
difficult to remember can have as much entropy as a "good" password
without any trouble, even if the passphrase system doesn't require a
verbatim match (for example, it may fold case) in order to accomodate
minor differences.  It's not hard for most people to remember a
quotation of a couple of sentences, for example.

It also helps to have a sensible threat model.  It may be acceptible
to keep a file of passwords on a computer, for example, if it's
properly protected; if that machine is sufficiently compromised to
allow an attacker to get the contents of the file, they can get the
secret information in other ways (eg a keystroke logger).  Absolute
security rules in the absence of a threat model are security theater,
and generally the sign that security policy is being set by someone
who knows nothing about the subject.

> HB> Or if they can, they use the same password everywhere.
> 
>     The latter makes sense, actually not the same password everywhere,  
> but a set of, say 5 userid/password pairs depending on the necessary  
> security level.

Or a single (or better handful of) passwords that are mangled
slightly, in a manner the user can reconstruct, for each login domain
- for example, the user appends a character he associates with the
site to the "base" password.  That adds a little security against
manual attacks (it's negligible for automated ones that are at all
sophisticated).

>    At least, nobody has yet cracked the passwords I use for my bank  
> accounts...

You mean, none of the people who have cracked them have yet used
them in ways you have noticed.
```

<http://search.ebay.com/thumb-drive_W0QQsofocusZbsQQsbrftogZ1QQfromZR10QQsacatZ-1QQcatrefZC6QQftrtZ1QQftrvZ1QQsadisZ200QQfposZQ5AIPQ2FPostalQQfsopZ1QQfsooZ1QQcoactionZcompareQQcopagenumZ1QQcoentrypageZsearch>
 and you'll even be able to set a postal code for more refined searches.

>Somebody in Future Shop demoed one to me while he was 
>discussing a new XP Machine, putting it into a USB slot to look at his 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>to. Not that I spend my time sauntering around computer stores.

I picked up one of those at a discount store (a US chain called Ross Dress 
for Less) but was dissatisfied with its weight, feel, and sensitivity... 
outside of that, of course, it was fine.  I received a gift of a Microsoft 
brand version and have been quite satisfied ever since... well, as 
satisfied as one can be with such a device... it takes my fingers away 
from the keyboard where they belong, you know.

```
On Fri, 18 Mar 2005 16:23:43 GMT, "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
wrote:

>The problem with passwords is a serious problem.   We can't use passwords that
>are easy to remember, and we can't write them down and post them next to our
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>harvest such passwords).

Software that's serious about security can't harvest your password
because it doesn't know your password. All it knows is a one-way
encrypted version. Unix is like that. Everyone can read the passwd
file but it's practically impossible to determine the cleartext
password. Java offers similar encryption using MD5 and SHA-x. 

Homegrown security written by applications programmers is always a
joke. I wish I had a dollar for every time I've seen a User table or
VSAM file that had userids and passwords in the clear. 

If a Web site can email your password when you click on Forgot
Password, it's not serious about security. Your cleartext password is
sitting in a database somewhere. A secure site will reset your
password to a random number, send that in a link and invalidate it
after the first use, which required you to choose a new one. 

Biometrics (DNA, retina, fingerprint) and implanted chips can be
forged easily if they're sent across an unencrypted network.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Passwords

_(reply depth: 46)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2005-03-19T21:13:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d1i4ll02jqd@news2.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<d0ncmi$d6$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <d0q0br$l49$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <ogp331hluvnoqc5ppt5kb9acc5r1tpeclc@4ax.com> <d0sqpf$j4b$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9r9631pbsbes9gl1ndqkgrllot8jnmsdp2@4ax.com> <kKIYd.654568$Xk.65709@pd7tw3no> <4bec4$42338828$45491f85$14816@KNOLOGY.NET> <WqNYd.656187$Xk.17621@pd7tw3no> <88439$42361b49$45491f85$26450@KNOLOGY.NET> <1HC_d.159830$qB6.40683@tornado.tampabay.rr.com> <d1evfn$ofh$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <89tm31h2l01dq59i7gu785pk3r385tkmdf@4ax.com>`

```

In article <89tm31h2l01dq59i7gu785pk3r385tkmdf@4ax.com>, Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> writes:
> 
> Software that's serious about security can't harvest your password
> because it doesn't know your password. All it knows is a one-way
> encrypted version.

Technically, that's not encryption; it's a verifier.  "One-way
encryption" is an oxymoron.  But yes, that's considerably better
than keeping the password in some reversable form.

> Unix is like that.

Sort of (if we're talking about traditional Unix user authentication
- typical modern Unix systems support multiple authentication
mechanisms, including some that work other ways).

The traditional Unix authentication mechanism does not store user
passwords in any form, true.  It stores, in effect, cryptographic
hashes of them.  (The original system used DES and a salt, as
cryptographic hashes weren't well-known at the time, but it's the
same idea.)

However, traditional Unix authentication requires that the
authenticating software have the password in the clear at some point
in order to rehash it and compare it with the stored hash.  That
means, for example, that for network authentication the plain-text
password is in flight, and if the channel isn't secure then the
password is exposed.

There are much better verifier mechanisms available now, based on
zero-knowledge proofs (eg SRP and PAK-RY) and similar (for these
purposes) mechanisms.  Those don't let a hostile server steal your
password, because they never see it; what the see is proof that you
know it.

> Everyone can read the passwd
> file but it's practically impossible to determine the cleartext
> password.

Whether it's "practically impossible" depends on rather more factors
than just the use of a verifier.  Offline dictionary attacks are
always possible.  Whether they're practical depends on the work
factor of the verification method, the entropy in the password, and
the resources available to the attacker.

Traditional Unix authentication does not do well here.  Modified
versions, such as the MD5 password hashes typically used in Linux
systems these days, are considerably better.  However, a weak
password will always be a weak password.

> Homegrown security written by applications programmers is always a
> joke.

I'm not sure about "always", but certainly security systems written
by people who don't know what they're doing are very unlikely to be
secure.  The ones written by people who *do* know what they're doing
often have holes, for that matter.

> VSAM file that had userids and passwords in the clear. 

That may be acceptable under the threat model that mechanism is meant
to address.  There are uses for so-called "honest user" and "kid
sister" modes in software security.  Sometimes it's enough to have a
strong argument that Bob couldn't have logged in as Alice without
doing something he's not allowed to do.

> Biometrics (DNA, retina, fingerprint) and implanted chips can be
> forged easily if they're sent across an unencrypted network.

True, and security research has consistently shown that current
biometric mechanisms are, in the large, crap anyway.  They've proven
easy to deceive, at least at reasonable cost/benefit ratios.

Biometrics are largely useful as security theater, at this point.
They discourage attackers who aren't very determined and they make
users feel secure.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Passwords

_(reply depth: 50)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2005-03-22T18:43:09+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d1povt02mbr@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<d0ncmi$d6$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <89tm31h2l01dq59i7gu785pk3r385tkmdf@4ax.com> <d1n05q$19q$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9TJmUmnPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <d1ncbs$80q$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <1111460663.843330.300120@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`

```

In article <1111460663.843330.300120@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> writes:
> > But the industry needs to recognize that the system doesn't
> > work.   MicroSoft has the power to do something about it,
> 
> Microsoft is the _problem_ not the solution.  Unix had expiring
> passwords and other mechanisms decades ago.

Expiring passwords are part of the problem, not a solution.  Expiring
passwords make authentication more cumbersome for users, which is a
bad thing (because it discourages secure practices) unless there's
significant security benefit; they make it much easier for users to
forget passwords, or to try to use memorable (and thus often guess-
able) ones; and in practice they've been shown to greatly increase the
opportunities for various social-engineering attacks.  For example,
IT help desks in organizations with password-expiration mechanisms
are often swamped with  password-reset requests (particularly if all
passwords across the organization expire simultaneously), and so do
little or nothing to verify them.

> MS barely requires passwords

True.  Microsoft's reluctance to implement any sort of security in
the "home" editions of its OS has done no one any favors.

> and implements them badly.

While I rarely defend Microsoft on security matters, the current MS
password-based authentication mechanisms are better than the ones
used in many extant Unix systems.  Windows has a standard mechanism
for network authentication which doesn't require flowing the secret,
for example.

> > maybe by coming out with a keyboard that has a thumb-print reader

Ack.  Biometrics are most definitely not the answer, at least not at
this time (and probably never, for many applications).  They have an
abysmal track record.  Attacks against them have nasty side effects
(who wants to lose a finger so that someone can impersonate them?).
They make revocation ... complicated.

> > and including some standard ways of producing verifications using
> > this that can be easily incorporated by web sites.    
> 
> SSL ?

SSL itself doesn't include a verification mechanism per se, but it
does have provision for using standard verification mechanisms like
X.509 certificates in a PKI.  SSL with bilateral certificate-based
authentication is a workable system, assuming decent PKI and
certificate hygiene.  And X.509 certs let you use decent-length
passphrases instead of idiotically-short passwords.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Passwords

_(reply depth: 52)_

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2005-03-23T17:54:58+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d1sahi024m1@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<d0ncmi$d6$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <89tm31h2l01dq59i7gu785pk3r385tkmdf@4ax.com> <d1n05q$19q$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9TJmUmnPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <d1ncbs$80q$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <1111460663.843330.300120@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <d1povt02mbr@news4.newsguy.com> <d1prgk$p9o$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```

In article <d1prgk$p9o$1@peabody.colorado.edu>, "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> writes:
> 
> On 22-Mar-2005, mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik) wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> have some suggestions?

Well, passphrases are already better than passwords.  That would be
suggestion number one.  Passwords are well-understood; passphrases
improve on them.

If you're looking for something other than passphrases, I'm not sure
I have anything so specific as a suggestion.  Tons of stuff has been
written on this topic; I've read a bunch, but certainly not a majority
of it, and my conclusion is that there is no silver bullet (which is
probably not a surprise).  No approach is best for all applications;
I don't even think there's any approach which is generally better than
the alternatives for most applications.

As Bruce Schneier says, there are three sources of authentication
information:

   - something you have (eg a physical key)
   - something you know (eg a password)
   - something you are (eg biometrics)

The third is actually a combination of the first two, in some ratio,
with some additional restrictions.  For example, a thumbprint is
"something you have", but it's impractical (from the user's point of
view) to transfer or revoke.  A signature is a combination of
"something you have" (the unconscious quirks and aspects of your
body that lead you to sign your name in a particular way) and
"something you know" (how you prefer to sign it), but it's possible
for a trained attacker to duplicate it without actually getting
possession of the "something you have" aspect.

That's one reason why biometrics are largely unsuccessful; they just
try to find a technology in the body that serves the roles played by
other, non-biometric authentication technologies.

So, if we drop the "something you are", we're left with "have" or
"know" or a combination of the two.  Smartcards typically use both.
That's useful for defending against attacks, but it leads to more
failure modes ("I'm on vacation and don't have my smartcard with
me").  And increased failure modes almost always improve the chances
for social engineering attacks against security administrators; the
more administrators get legitimate requests to bypass security, the
more they're likely to honor illegitimate ones.

And, of course, as soon as you add a physical token you increase your
infrastructure costs, usually significantly.

While there are definitely many applications where the threat model
justifies the increased cost and fragility of adding a physical token
like a smartcard, for ordinary login purposes I'm not very keen on
them.

Also, note they do nothing to address a lot of the more probable
attacks against, say, a company's internal network.  First, most
attackers are insiders, so they'll already have authentication;
they're more likely mounting privilege-escalation attacks.  Second,
smartcards don't help if an authenticated user's system is compro-
mised, which is far more likely an attack against the typical
Windows user.  An attacker who has remote control over a machine
that's already authenticated and connected to the network doesn't
care how that authentication was achieved.  Third, as I noted above,
they don't help against social engineering attacks, which are the
most successful class of outsider attacks.
```

No, actually, my grade for JFK was due to several reasons.  First, his 
vision of a moon landing was great - it energized the scientific 
community with something positive.  Second was his excellent playing of 
that chess match known as the Cuban Missile Crisis, successfully 
averting nuclear war on our own land.  Third, his civil-rights work, 
encouraging those such as Dr. Martin Luther King Jr.  (On MLK day, there 
are two folks whose pictures are put up in the dining hall on base - MLK 
and JFK.)

And, who can beat the line "Ask not what your country can do for you, 
but what you can do for your country"?  Man, if *that* isn't a smack in 
the face of today's victim society, I don't know what is.

On the other side, I thought the whole Camelot thing was dumb - there's 
no royalty in this country.  And, his extramarital dalliances detracted 
some from some folks' opinions of him.  He was taken too early, and I 
think our country is the worse for it.

unintentionally wrong.  Forgetting meeting someone is understandable - 
premeditated perjury is not.  And, just because he met him but said he 
didn't *doesn't* mean that his campaign coordinated with the Swift Boat 
Veterans for Truth.

In fact, one of his volunteer legal advisers resigned during the

and he had answered it.  Even though what he did *was* legal under the 
new election laws we had this past year, he resigned to avoid the 
appearance of impropriety.

because you're liberal, he's green, she's a commie, he's a socialist, 
and the other guy is a libertarian - we can still be friends, and we can 
still talk COBOL!

> Jimmy - no more below

HEY!!  There's a whole 'nother *country* below you...  ;)

Howard Brazee wrote:
> On 12-Mar-2005, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:
> 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> comfortable with.   Someone that talks like I do.

If I interpret the last sentence correctly - very astute. I'm talking as 
a 'foreigner' from the UK where Canucks and Yanks sound the same. Rather 
like the reaction to Ozzies and Kiwis. As we know both 'sets' are very 
different.

I get the sense that there is a Middle America accent - certainly down 
the top part of the East Coast, NY City being 'distinct' and those NH 
sounds from JFK. West coast - there's no such thing as an accent is 
there - unless you include Arnie :-).

LBJ - reminded me of the line from 'Oklahoma !', "The corn is as high as 
an elephant's thigh.....". Presidential rating - have to give him a good 
one - inherited JFK's 'Nam mess and took on the civil rights issue as 
several others have detailed.

Jimmy Carter - my personal favourite. What a real hayseed he sounded 
like. Got low marks from US. As he has said himself subsequently, "Had I 
done some sabre rattling over the Iran hostages, I could possibly have 
been re-elected'. Accent or no, he was a sincere man, and his good works 
are really post-administration like Habitat and his Carter Institute 
supervising 'democratic' votes in Third World countries.

George Bush Senior. Liked him. Not a particularly distinct Texan accent, 
although it is there.

Billy Boy. Again another hayseed accent. His performance - well he got 
rid of the debt, but overall the jury is still out. To my mind, he had 
much more intellectualism than most. See him being questioned now, out 
comes a lucid, easily framed reply. That initial reaction to his 
'accent' - now it conveys some charm - sure didn't hurt him with women.

GWB - not just me but many Canucks when questioned, and without 
*knowing* there political bent. He maybe daddy's son, but oh boy, that 
Texan twang coupled with four word sentences. Still I'll give him his 
due - he looks 'presidential', is a snappy dresser before the podium, 
and I love his choice of ties (neck wear) :-)

Jimmy
```

> church does not condemn homosexuals, (Forgive the sinner but not the

I see the starting quote, but not the end one.   The interesting aspect about
gay marriages to me is the observation that there's no way it can have the
impact that allowing divorce had.     And anybody who can find Biblical
reference to disallow gay marriages but can't find Biblical references to
disallow divorce doesn't use scripture to guide him.

But I'm a believer that the state should get out of the marriage business.   If
my S.O. is my ailing parent, I should be able to claim him as my tax or
insurance partner.   I shouldn't have to have sex with him.   (The state has
already separated out the issues of parenthood from marriage).    With this in
line, marriage returns to being a religious ceremony, and none of the state's
business.

- **Message-ID:** `<d14nlk$2i2$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<255j215394bc0u68ci6m9sde4vvc2g8oqh@4ax.com> <1110823768.373645.168100@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`

```

On 14-Mar-2005, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

> While a marriage may include a religious ceremony, and/or a drunken
> party, and many other things, the _actual_ mearriage is the signing of
> the register which is entirely the state's business, and may need
> nothing more.

Not according to the church.

Why should the state care?   Because it has contracts that are only applicable
to married people - such as social security spouse benefits?    Fixable.   Let
me pick my own domestic partner for that.   Let me pick my autistic brother, my
crippled neighbor, or the beautiful woman who lives down the street.   It's my
money they're taxing, how it gets spent should not be dependent upon my sex
life.

Or is it because marriage defines how the state defines children and parental
responsibilities?    Demonstratively false.

Military personnel get paid more for being married.   Do they earn more being
married?

The real reason in the U.S. is because when churches were allowed to define
marriage, the LDS allowed polygamy.   The state felt churches couldn't be
trusted to follow the state's morality standards, so it took over.   Eventually
it would have done so anyway - grab power where you can, but it wasn't done for
the good of the governed.

The best thing about the state running marriage is because I get to tell the
following story:

When I was in the USAF, I knew someone who celebrated 3 wedding anniversaries.
 He was stationed in Korea and married his fiance in Hawaii.   She moved into
married people's quarters and his pay went up.   A month later, he returned to
Hawaii, where their families met and they had a big church wedding.    Since
they were on opposite sides of the international date line when they got
married, when I knew them, they celebrated his anniversary, her anniversary, and
their anniversary every year.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: US Presidents and Foreign Policy

_(reply depth: 45)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-03-14T13:45:00-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1110836700.322931.165240@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<d14s91$52u$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<255j215394bc0u68ci6m9sde4vvc2g8oqh@4ax.com> <1110830907.944895.107690@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <d14s91$52u$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
> My marriage isn't between my wife, my state and myself,

Well, actually it is.  The state may have laws or other devices that
grant priviliges or impose restrictions that apply to people who are
'married'.  If you want to be in that categorey then you enter into a
state sanctioned contract that puts you in that categorey, otherwise
you don't.

If you want to have a private contract between yourself and your wife
that does not involve the state, then do that, but don't expect the
state to be a party to it.

> That little piece of paper in the courthouse should not
> be the determinant of our marriage status.

It is only the determinant of whether you are entitled to those items
for which 'married' people are entitled.  If you want to just live
together and change your name then who cares if you sign up or not.

> It was done for the _average_ good.

> Is the average person better off that he can't define his crippled
brother as
> his social security beneficiary?

No, but the 'average person' may be better off if _someone_else_ can't
define a crippled brother as his social security beneficiary (by, for
example, not having to pay the taxes that contribute to those
payments).

> Who benefits by this requirement that we have
> official sex in order to gain governmental benefits?

No one _requires_ you to have sex with anyone else, or indeed not to
except where explicitly disallowed.  You can be married and not have
sex, you can be not married and have sex.

> For it to be an average good, there must be some real benefits to
having the state limit
> these benefits to those who pay the $50 fee to legally copulate.

If copulation outside marriage between consenting hetrosexual adults is
against any actual law then I would suggest that you change the law or
change your state.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: US Presidents and Foreign Policy

_(reply depth: 47)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-03-14T15:27:32-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1110842852.048727.51950@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<d151r6$826$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<255j215394bc0u68ci6m9sde4vvc2g8oqh@4ax.com> <1110836700.322931.165240@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <d151r6$826$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
> OK what is the "general good" that results from the state defining
marriage?

There may, or may not be, a general good there at all, but that is not
the point.

Once it is established that there is a thing that is marriage (by
whatever means), then it is in the general good that it be formalised
and recorded.  For example if there is a principle that 'a wife cannot
testify against a husband (and vv)', then there is a general good that
this not be arbitrary.  If someone can claim anyone as 'wife' by merely
saying it then this may defeat the course of justice.

> Social Security benefits should not be limited to those whose
contractual
> obligation is matrimonial.   (Non-married people pay just as much SS
tax).

Some benefits may be _less_ if the people are married.  For example a
single female may get unemployment benefit while a married woman living
with an employed husband may not.

> Bachelor soldiers serve the country as well as married soldiers do.

That may, or may not, be true.  For example married men may not sign
up, or may leave as soon as possible if they can't afford their
families.  OTOH bachelor soldiers may cause more problems when posted
to other countries, or even other towns.
```

Howard Brazee wrote:
> On 14-Mar-2005, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

Had to go back and re-read Donald and his 'copulating' I disagree. I 
think the issue really is a large number of modernists see that the 
definition of the word 'marriage' should be changed to cover the sorts 
of things you listed above.

Although in some other threads, others have disputed 'marriage' from 
earliest days, it has been a tenet of many cultures and religions. I'll 
accept Richard's concept, "They've all got it wrong, it's just 
mythology". Nevertheless, that's how a fair proportion of the world 
thinks, although 'church-going' has been on a decline since 1900 onwards 
- but see below.

I don't know whether it got any coverage in US, but there was a small 
reference to it in BBC World News. A while back Bill stated he was happy 
that (I think), the local Evangelical bishop was gay. (Evangelical = 
Anglican = Church of England). Do you know where the greatest fuss came 
from ? Africa - they (the local African Anglicans) are very unhappy 
campers. Why ? They had tribal traditions which put a specific meaning 
on 'marriage'. Along come their colonial masters with the Union Jack, 
Tricolor or whatever, impose on them a European process of government, 
and as a side bonus also introduce Christianity.

 From yesterday's paper, "The pews of Africa's churches now hold 390 
million worshipers - more than three times the total of 35 years ago. 
Over the next two decades. Africa's congregation is likely to grow by 
another 200 million ... focus is moving away from the empty churches of 
Europe.... we 'Whites' account for only 43 percent ...Nigeria, the home 
of the world's second-largest Anglican congregation.....a potential 
Papal candidate is Cardinal Francis Arinze of Nigeria, leading a flock 
of 147 million Catholics.........".

The point of the article is the shift of focus. N. America (US and 
Canada) are already on a collision course with 'mainstream' Anglicanism, 
starting point being Bill's bishop. How do you think the same church 
leaders are going to react to the changing of the meaning of the word 
'marriage'.

Now here's where I disagree with Donald, and having been brought up a 
catholic and probably opted out when he was a teenager, hopefully he can 
follow me. I can only express it from a catholic point of view. Let's 
first cast aside all the historical hypocrisy and wheeling and dealing 
in the Catholic Church. I'm referring to catholicism with a start date 
if you like, of post WW2. Marriage is seen as a spiritual union, 
triggered by a common attraction, resulting in love, then formalized in 
marriage. The species must continue - so like other animals we 
procreate. Catholicism doesn't disagree with the edict from the Jewish 
Good Book, "Thou shalt not commit adultery". No ambiguity there - "Thou 
shalt not have thy jollies with a women who takes your fancy; but only 
with thine lawful wife". (Minor problem - some of those early patriarchs 
had more than one wife; then the two daughters who got the old guy drunk 
so that they could 'lie' with him to continue his seed). Nevertheless 
the commandment is fairly specific.

Worth remembering, and going back quite a few centuries, catholic 
theologians were very uncomfortable with sex, but saw it as a 'necessary 
evil' to  procreate. I think late 1870's but the Catholic Curs went for 
"marriage is about procreation" and has consistently followed in that 
vein, "no contraception", although, (I have no idea of numbers) probably 
50-80 % plus 'do their own thing' using a condom. The church's attitude 
is that your female partner is not a rubber dolly that you take out of 
the closet, pump some air into and have your 'jollies' with as you see 
fit. Well we all like *IT* - we don't use rubber dollies, but the real 
thing !

Take it or leave it, that's one perspective and not too far removed if 
you scratch a practicing Christian, Jew, Muslim, Bhuddist, Sikh or 
whatever. So when it comes to issues like same sex marriage they are all 
up in arms. Certainly one rabbi has been quoted as saying he will hand 
in his Registrar's card - if pushed, and on Canada's East coast some 
denominationals have already quit as registrars. But morality aside, 
note that their major objection to same-sex partnerships being
legalized is the wedge they see being used to attack their centuries old 
meaning of the word 'marriage'.

I can quite happily live with homosexuality PROVIDING you do NOT try to 
jump mine or my son's bones ! Nor will I accept, in the spirit of 
political correctness, that I must accept your homosexual definition of 
the word 'marriage'. (Let's be real - that's certainly the way it 
appears to be going).

Last message I said Howard 'muddied the waters'. This time he has cast a 
fog on the issue - a real pea-souper. I accept quite happily that 
homosexual partnerships should have legal rights to property etc. This 
time around he wants to extend the issue to each orang-utang or 
chimpanzee hanging on his family tree. (Actually I *really* like both 
those apes :-) ). If he sees injustices currently, then by all means 
pursue political motivation to cure the apparent ills. No quibble from 
me providing you don't use the word 'marriage' - which has a specific 
dictionary meaning to me. God knows what the term should be, 'Civil 
Union', 'Human Partnership Act/Bill', or whatever.

Leave me with 'marriage' and you pick and choose your new term and 
legalize it.

Keep on trying to shove 'marriage' to mean other than its age old 
interpretation both culturally and in a religious sense, then you are on 
a collision course with 'believers'. All too apparent if you look at the 
last presidential election. Compromise lads, compromise. We, both sides, 
could come out of this as winners. ('Winners' is a bit overboard - but 
certainly no losers).

Jimmy

How very perceptive of you, Mr Brazee... it wasn't offered as an argument, 
it was offered as a statement of how the law, as Law, changes the way 
folks deal with each other.  Good law, bad law... it doesn't matter, it 
still is The Law and carries full weight.

>
>What is and what should be aren't always the same thing.  

I don't believe I suggested otherwise, Mr Brazee... but what do I know?

DD

> The state claims that our social security benefits aren't a tax, but
are

They _are_ paid for by taxes, they _are_ an 'investment' because the
alternative (such as 10 x police cost to thwart riots by starving
peasants) would be more expensive.

Please adjust your tinfoil hat to suit.

> Why not require your private company to pay more money to
> married people?   I think bald people should get paid more.

That's the thing. _Your_ opinions in this are irrelevant (or only as
relevant as a single vote).  The majority of employers have decided
that bald people don't get paid more, and they pay more for whatever
skills are short and in demand.

In the case of the military they pay more for married people because
they think they _are_ more valuable, or because they would not be able
to recruit them if they didn't.  In other words they have an actual
_reason_ for doing that (which may be mainly historical), while you
merely have a winge.

> Equality can be achieved in many ways.

> under the law?

Next you will be complaining that some people are forced to go to
school because of that piece of paper that is a birth certificate, or
are prevented from driving, or are forced to retire, or excluded from
public houses or not allowed to have sex.  All these things are agist
and treat people unequally.

> Why not require your private company to pay more money to
> married people?

Now some companies _may_ do that, there is nothing to stop them. In
fact I worked for a company that sent me overseas for a few months and,
because I was married and had a child, they paid for my wife and
child's fares too.

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-8D973E.21124719032005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> In article <113ovvhs3eg9725@corp.supernews.com>,
>  "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> corresponding reduction in taxes.

It so happens that I have a copy of Title 26 on my computer;
don't ask why!

26 USC Sec. 3101 "Rate of tax" has the two subsections
    (a) Old-age, survivors, and disability insurance, and
    (b) Hospital insurance.

26 USC Sec. 3111 "Rate of tax" is also divided into two
subsections as in 3101.

The difference is that 3101 is the tax paid by employees and
3111 is the tax paid by employers. Both sections are part of
Chapter 21, Federal Insurance Contributions Act

The 1965 amendment 'split' the tax.

      1965 - Pub. L. 89-97, Sec. 321(b), divided the total tax imposed
    under the entire section upon income through a tax equal to
    percentages of wages into two separate taxes by dividing the
    section into subsecs. (a) and (b), with subsec. (a) reflecting the
    tax for old-age, survivors, and disability insurance and subsec.
    (b) reflecting the tax for hospital insurance, ...

The 1983 amendment,

      1983 - Subsec. (a). Pub. L. 98-21 substituted table of rates for
    former pars. (1) to (7) which had imposed a tax on the income of
    every individual (1) with respect to wages received ...

raised the OASDI rates to the following:

     1984, 1985, 1986, or 1987         5.7 percent
     1988 or 1989                      6.06 percent
     1990 or thereafter                6.2 percent.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-F4E423.10573420032005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> In article <113q2a33gigik7a@corp.supernews.com>,
>  "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > > > So one might conclude that FICA is a tax that in part pays
insurance
> > > > > premiums.  And we must also adhere to the fact that FICA stands
for, "
> > > > > Federal Insurance Contributions Act".  There's that word
"insurance"
> > > > > again.  But FICA is also known as the Social Security Tax.
> > > >
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> Medicare is not part of your 6.2 percent.  It hasn't been for 15 years.

What I showed from the text of the 1965 amendment was that
the Hospital insurance (Medicare) has had its own rate since 1965
(nearly 40 years) and that the 1983 amendment to FICA increased
the OASDI rate while leaving the hospital rate alone.

Here is the complete text from 3101 (3111 is similar), which shows
thirty years of separate rates for Medicare.

-STATUTE-
    (a) Old-age, survivors, and disability insurance
      In addition to other taxes, there is hereby imposed on the income
    of every individual a tax equal to the following percentages of the
    wages (as defined in section 3121(a)) received by him with respect
    to employment (as defined in section 3121(b)) -

    ---------------------------------------------------------------------
    In cases of wages received during: The rate shall be:
    ---------------------------------------------------------------------
     1984, 1985, 1986, or 1987         5.7 percent
     1988 or 1989                      6.06 percent
     1990 or thereafter                6.2 percent.
                     -------------------------------
    (b) Hospital insurance
      In addition to the tax imposed by the preceding subsection, there
    is hereby imposed on the income of every individual a tax equal to
    the following percentages of the wages (as defined in section
    3121(a)) received by him with respect to employment (as defined in
    section 3121(b)) -
        (1) with respect to wages received during the calendar years
      1974 through 1977, the rate shall be 0.90 percent;
        (2) with respect to wages received during the calendar year
      1978, the rate shall be 1.00 percent;
        (3) with respect to wages received during the calendar years
      1979 and 1980, the rate shall be 1.05 percent;
        (4) with respect to wages received during the calendar years
      1981 through 1984, the rate shall be 1.30 percent;
        (5) with respect to wages received during the calendar year
      1985, the rate shall be 1.35 percent; and
        (6) with respect to wages received after December 31, 1985, the
      rate shall be 1.45 percent.
    (c) Relief from taxes in cases covered by certain international
        agreements
      During any period in which there is in effect an agreement
    entered into pursuant to section 233 of the Social Security Act
    with any foreign country, wages received by or paid to an
    individual shall be exempt from the taxes imposed by this section
    to the extent that such wages are subject under such agreement to
    taxes or contributions for similar purposes under the social
    security system of such foreign country.

This is the text of the 1977 amendment, which is the last noted
change for subsection (b).

      1977 - Subsec. (a). Pub. L. 95-216, Sec. 101(a)(1), substituted
    [...].
      Subsec. (b). Pub. L. 95-216, Sec. 101(b)(1), substituted ''wages
    received during the calendar year 1978, the rate shall be 1.00
    percent'' for ''wages received during the calendar years 1978
    through 1980, the rate shall be 1.10 percent'' in par. (2),
    substituted ''wages received during the calendar years 1979 and
    1980, the rate shall be 1.05 percent'' for ''wages received during
    the calendar years 1981 through 1985, the rate shall be 1.35
    percent'', in par. (3), substituted ''wages received during the
    calendar years 1981 through 1984, the rate shall be 1.30 percent''
    for ''wages received after December 31, 1985, the rate shall be
    1.50 percent'' in par. (4), and added pars. (5) and (6).

There is a discrepancy between the text shown for the amendment
and the text of the statute. The statute shows lower rates. I can not
explain the discrepancy. Perhaps, only substantive changes of
amendments are shown and an amendment, after 1977, reducing
the rates for hospital insurance was not considered substantive.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Social Security

_(reply depth: 55)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2005-03-18T19:40:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d1fb19$35u$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<255j215394bc0u68ci6m9sde4vvc2g8oqh@4ax.com> <1110912158.676799.165270@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <d17bfs$ldn$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <5583a$4237957a$45491f85$14182@KNOLOGY.NET> <d191kk$dvp$1@panix5.panix.com> <joe_zitzelberger-23003F.09392918032005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <113m8rpp5uj8ifa@corp.supernews.com>`

```
The Social Security money is a tax because it is a mandatory payment of money
from us to the government.   That is not sufficient reason for it not to also be
an insurance payment.

Politically, its design was for it to be an insurance program, and that's how it
was sold.    You can be currently insured if you are paying in now, or fully
insured if you have paid 40 quarters.

One big difference between Social Security and a mandatory private insurance
program is that private companies have more stringent fiduciary requirements.  
Another big difference is that the politicians set the rates, not businessmen.
 The third big difference is that the money taken in by SS taxes is spent by the
federal government.   If there is a surplus, more tanks can be purchased.  
There is some accounting going back and forth, but that surplus is taken into
account when determining our deficit.    The line between Social Security and
the rest of the Federal government has been getting more and more blurry over
the decades.   Nobody talks about the military going bankrupt.   The Military,
social security, Medicare, etc.  are all federal expenditures that are paid for
by taxes.

With the sales job that social security is an investment, there should never
have been people complaining about double dipping.   So what if we have a
government job or a military job that invests in a separate retirement plan?  
What we pay for should be what we get.    It is interesting that illegal aliens
pay social security into fake accounts, but are unlikely to get that money back.
  If it were a retirement plan, they should get what they paid for.

The "what we pay is what we get" doesn't mean that married people should get
more than non married people get.    Self employed people appear to pay more
than employed people do - but this is a fiction.   When your company pays half
of your social security, it is really paying all of it as part of the cost of
hiring you - and mentioning half of it on your paycheck.    But people believe
that they are only paying half of this tax.
```

> And Richard, "Myth".

Please do not misrepresent what I may, or may not, say.

In many cases much of what is mythology and/or (what are known today
as) religious writing may well be based on actual people and happening.
 The fact is that it is likely that many christians would refer to,
say, the Greek Pantheon or the Hindu dhama, as mythology while
maintaining that _their_ writing were true.

I see nothing to choose between these, and many others.  Parts are
myths and parts are most likely based on real (natural) people and
events, but exagerated and confused by being passed down orally.

For parts of the writings I see no reason, for example, why people of a
few thousand years ago could not have been as inventive in their story
telling as the many thousands of authors are today. Noah's Ark is just
the Star Trek of C20BCE.

```

On 15-Mar-2005, "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

> > Not according to the church.
>
> Which "the church"?  "The Church of Jesus Christ" recognizes the simplest of
> legally-binding civil ceremonies as having as much weight as a wedding
> celebrated by the highest-ranking minister in the denomination.

And Roman Catholics have this as the one sacrament that doesn't require a Priest
to perform.   But most, if not all churches recognize non-church marriages for a
very practical reason - otherwise people who don't have access to clergy can't
get married.    But these non-church marriages do not give special authority to
the state.   The marriage can be performed by virtually anybody (or just by the
couple themselves), and does not have to be accepted by the state in order to be
valid.

And many, if not most churches do not accept *all* marriages that all states
accept.

Another thing that I don't like to see is people who decide their marital status
based upon such things as Social Security benefits.    Don't make it a legal
issue what an older couple decide to call their status.   If marriage is a
sacrament - then it's not the government's business.   If it's just what people
call themselves, then it also should not determine legal status.

Interesting - legally if a couple in Colorado represent themselves as married -
they are married.   But I haven't heard of anybody losing social security
benefits by doing so.   It will effect inheritance and can require divorce to
end though.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: US Presidents and Foreign Policy

_(reply depth: 45)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-03-15T10:49:17-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d17and$qde$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<255j215394bc0u68ci6m9sde4vvc2g8oqh@4ax.com> <1110823768.373645.168100@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <d14nlk$2i2$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <d174mh$mgv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <d1769l$ig9$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:d1769l$ig9$1@peabody.colorado.edu...

> And Roman Catholics have this as the one sacrament that doesn't require a
Priest
> to perform.

No, I don't think that's quite right.  I think there's more than one.

The Bull "Exultate Deo" of Pope Eugene IV (1388-1447) states, in part, on
baptism:  "The minister of this sacrament is the priest, to whom it belongs
to baptize by reason of his office.  In case of necessity, however, not only
a priest or deacon, but even a layman or woman, nay, even a pagan or heretic
can baptize, provided he observes the form used by the Church and intends to
perform what the Church performs. ..."

Note also that there are two sacraments a mere priest isn't allowed to
perform:  confirmation and ordination. Both require a bishop.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

jce wrote:

Thank goodness for Auntie Beeb. Following is a set of statistics I've 
always wanted to find. Better go looking at the BBC for other info.

> 
> Source: bbc.
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Canadian figures sure look small at 0.3%, considering they 'policed' the 
Atlantic for merchant convoys and their bomber crews served as part of 
Bomber Command. (In case I miss it and he protests - Donald's dad was an 
RCAF fighter pilot).

One of those nice reminiscing articles in yesterday's paper. Two ladies, 
now 80 plus, recounting their days back in the 40's - one served as a 
parachute packer.

Canada as a whole was quite useful for air training but Alberta 
particularly for the southern landscape, (wide, open and clear skies), 
flowing down to Montana. We had the Commonwealth Air Training Program 
with some 18 flying schools in Alberta. Crews were trained from 23 
countries. (My parents next-door-neighbour's son did his navigation 
training here - hated it ! Back then Calgary looked like the set for the 
Gary Cooper movie 'High Noon'). BTW one lady made specific reference 
that the Aussies wore a black uniform - Somebody from Oz-land is that 
still the case or do they wear an 'RAF' blue.

As one of the ladies said Calgary was then awash in military uniforms, 
more than any other city in Canada. She recalls VE or VJ-Day - she 
didn't specify which, where they went snaking through downtown Calgary 
in a joyous conga. Then puff ! Suddenly all the lads were gone.

Reading Daniel and Robert - I think they have got so much wrong about 
Iraq. Presidents get voted in on domestic issues, ('How much are you 
going to tax us ?") - who gives a damn about foreign policy. It takes 
just too much typing to explain - so I'll give it a miss.

Guys forget Iraq - it's smalltime, as probably are Iran and N. Korea.
How's about this, just like Richard pointed out the cause for the Pearl 
Harbor attack :-

'To ensure oil supplies, the Peoples Republic will probably have to go 
to war with the USA by 2008".

Sez who ? The party sponsored Chinese Military publishing house.

Jimmy, Calgary AB

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Treeviews

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-03-02T17:07:39+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38ksggF5opqicU1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7iLSd.472515$8l.105860@pd7tw1no> <cvo5k2$mas$1@panix5.panix.com> <1109446377.120385.263750@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <cvvlgu$838$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <38j1h3F5ocqh7U1@individual.net> <nx3Vd.540924$8l.239854@pd7tw1no>`

```

"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message
news:nx3Vd.540924$8l.239854@pd7tw1no...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
> > "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> > investigation, I don't care. It is worth waiting for. Hurried solutions
are
> > half arsed solutions that require constant attention and cost more in
the
> > long run than doing it properly in the first place.)
> >
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I s'pose I should really 'fess up that "do it once; do it right" was an
official Government slogan in New Zealand back in the 70's.

There was concern in some circles that the  laid back Kiwi attitude of
"She'll be right..." was not helping our perceived efficiency in the foreign
markets we were seeking to penetrate. It is pretty hard to change a National
characteristic, but several million dollars of taxpayers money were
funnelled into intensive advertising and training campaigns.  I don't know
how effective it was (how would you measure it?), but it is certainly true
that today we are recognised as efficient and skilful in the things we
export (mainly movies... <g>), and we are managing to work our way back to
prosperity without dependence on sheep and cattle only.

It isn't always possible to 'do it once; do it right', but it is certainly
worth trying for, and there is no doubt in my mind that it beats the 'fix
what you can see and get it working until the next time it breaks down'
approach.

Pete.
```

RW> Here is an example from real life. A computer company has a system
RW> to process subscriptions for one magazine.  Then the salesman signs
RW> up another magazine, which requires very similar processing but a few
RW> minor differences in logic, for instance the way credits are given
RW> for cancellations. A REAL Cobol programmer wouldn't even consider
RW> rules tables; the answer would be obvious -- copy the program(s) to
RW> another name and change a few lines of code in the 'new' program.

   This is not special to COBOL programmers. I have seen similar  
things being done by Pascal programmers.

   The only difference might be, that a COBOL programmer would defend  
this as a matter of personal style, and not a question of good or bad  
programming.

> but the technique favored by REAL Cobol programmers is not callable
subroutines
> nor copybooks, it is copy-and-paste.

> Here is an example from real life.

Once again you take _one_ example and apply that to _all_, in this
case, all 'REAL Cobol programmers', whatever that is supposed to mean.
Does it distinguish them from artificial Cobol programmers or Integer
Cobol programmers ?

> A REAL Cobol programmer wouldn't even consider rules tables;

It seems to me that you are being "Dogmatic and Intollerant".
Programmers must follow The Robert Wagner Way into his brilliance.

Personally, I much prefer to have one more-complicated program that
will handle a variety of similar tasks rather than several different
programs.  That does not mean that there are not reasons to have
programs that do much the same but for different clients.  For example
it may be that the contract _requires_ that each customer have a
separate system so that the data and programs are actually owned by
them.

> It's an inside joke meaning job security

You are just bitter because you don't have any.
```

```

On 26-Feb-2005, Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:

> Yes, Cobol has reusable code, but the technique favored by REAL Cobol
> programmers is not callable subroutines nor copybooks, it is
> copy-and-paste.

Skipping the insult, there is good reason to sometimes prefer this technique.

In most CoBOL shops, the standards of how complete the testing must be is that
everything that runs that code must be completely tested.   This just doesn't
work when that code is run by a wide variety of programs.    So a CoBOL shop has
a choice - either accept the standards used by the OO people, or duplicate code.
  Redundant systems are less vulnerable.

It appears that the future is with lowered standards.
```

- **Date:** 2005-03-02T17:29:19+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38ktp3F5ph2ucU1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7iLSd.472515$8l.105860@pd7tw1no> <1109354625.353288.324170@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <cvnq4u$n5m$1@panix5.panix.com> <1109361297.008867.304890@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <cvo5k2$mas$1@panix5.panix.com> <1109369427.368192.123030@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <889121ppm9jcnu6tii0glcuetkvi1vnf4g@4ax.com> <cvvlbv$833$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3ul721hctc4dh0e0267b3u9uc460340ho3@4ax.com> <d02382$num$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:d02382$num$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 28-Feb-2005, Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote:
>
>
> Mainframe testing standards are designed around the high cost of being
wrong.
> Internet testing standards are designed around the high cost of being
late.
>

A very succinct,  though not entirely accurate, statement.

It is not entirely accurate because it includes two generalizations.

NOT ALL mainframe testing standards are designed around the high cost of
being wrong, and NOT ALL internet testing standards are designed around the
high cost of being late.

(nevertheless, it was a very nice turn of phrase, Howard... <g>)

In fact, some testing  standards in both camps are not concerned with the
costs of tardiness, inaccuracy , or any form of cost at all.

They simply want a standard. It makes them feel better, and when people ask,
they can say, "Well, of course, we're a professional shop so we have our
testing standards...". (Besides, everybody knows that standards are a Good
Thing, so better have some...).

That's why there are good and bad standards.

Often the testing standards are bypassed because there isn't time to do
comprehensive regression testing (when it is actually needed) and this
happens in mainframe shops just the same as it does with  web development.

The cost of being wrong in a web environment can be just as devastating as
it is in a mainframe environment (especially if it is credit card processing
that wasn't exhaustively tested.)

Good shops in both camps will consider all of the costs of failure (both in
terms of time and accuracy) and develop a proper testing plan accordingly;
it is not the prerogative of one type of shop to get it right (or wrong).

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Treeviews

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-27T14:08:49+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<38cktfF5l4mtrU1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7iLSd.472515$8l.105860@pd7tw1no> <1109361297.008867.304890@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <cvo5k2$mas$1@panix5.panix.com> <1109369817.028632.317290@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <cvpslc$5r0$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:cvpslc$5r0$1@panix5.panix.com...
> In article <1109369817.028632.317290@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>,
> Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> >> The original post states 'my teacher said dialogue is the correct verb
> >> since the purpose of the "dialog" box is to converse with the
end-user.';
> >
> >It also had:
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I watched this exchange with some amusement.  As usual the Doc has an
impeccable argument backed up by good authorities.

There is no doubt you are correct, Doc.

However, my sympathies here lie with Richard.

See, those of us who love the ENGLISH language (and this is not to decry or
diminish the AMERICAN dialect of it...which is perfectly valid, (especially
if you live in America)), ...we do find it discordant to hear words we
normally expect to be nouns, used as verbs.

I would have taken Richard's position myself, because I have never, until
this discussion, heard 'dialogue' used as a verb. But, in America, it has
been common practice for around 50 years now, to push nouns into use as
verbs.

It isn't 'right' or 'wrong', it just is.

To an English English speaker, it grates. But so what?

The fact that the OED now recognises it (and did in 1971), simply shows that
the scholars at Oxford are resigned to the changing nature of language and
see no reason not to make it official. (I do not dispute your authorities
here, Doc - I couldn't <G> - but I would be extremely surprised if an OLDER
edition of the OED - say, prior to 1930 gave the same definition...)

The fact of the matter is that language is an evolving and changing thing.
Local mores with regard to language do not necessarily constitute abuse.
American English is just as valid as English English, but some of us (self
included) need to remember that it is NOT English English and should not be
subject to the same judgements we make about our own speech.

I have adopted 'Americanisms' unconsciously into my own speech that would
make my English-born father turn in his grave, but I think I could justify
their use to the old boy, were he still with us... <g>.

On a long plane flight once, a fellow-traveller asked politely what my
accent was. Before I could reply, my English-born wife interjected with
heavy cynicism: "It's mid-Atlantic Antipodean..."  We laughed, but I knew
what she meant. She was resentful that my frequent sojourns in the US had
influenced my speech.  (Kiwi she would accommodate as "refeshing and
quaint", but Americanisms? no way...) Stating that now, I see how totally
irrational it was.

Churchill's famous quote summed it up nicely: "Britain and America: two
great countries divided by a common language"

Pete.
```
